It wasn't until after reading the reviews here and elsewhere that I realized this album isn't revered by most of the prog-rock Genesis fans.<br />Phil Collins and Steve Hackett weren't members of the band yet but that doesn't mean that the playing and the songs on this album aren't solid as hell.<br />In fact it has some of my favorite songs on it, I personally think \\"Stagnation\\" is one of the greatest Genesis songs ever.<br />The sound of the album is that of early 70's prog-rock, not too heavy most of the time, with long flourishing instrumental interludes peppered with Peter Gabriels' endlessly interesting lyrics and vocal delivery.<br />The production is muddy like most people say but that doesn't stop me from enjoying it.<br />If you are here in the first place you must be musically adventurous so why not give this album a try?<br />You could always go over to Spotify and listen to the entire album before you buy it.

0375704027|5.0|This book has nothing to do with Norway or woods.  Well, maybe with the woods, setting-wise.  It does, however, mention the Beatles song a couple of times.  (Okay, that's a lame opener.)<br /><br />Japanese author Haruki Murakami is known for his complex and surreal style of writing, tackling rather heavy societal issues in his fiction writing. His characters feel grounded and relatable.  His themes are, at times, controversial, yet people respond to them.<br /><br />Norwegian Wood is a coming-of-age story of Toru Watanabe, a young man in the 1960s-70s.  Toru narrates his story in first person struggling with his friends, school, and love life; dealing with suicide, relationships, and his own personal issues.  His best friend, Kizuki commits suicide and this event heavily affects Toru and the people around him.  He shares this world with Naoko, Kizuki's girlfriend, whom he is sort of in love with also.  Naoko never really recovers and goes into depression. Through the years, their lives are touched by more people: Midori, Toru's friend from school; Reiko, Naoko's roommate; and other colorful characters.<br /><br />The Beatles song that shares the same name as the novel describes Toru and Naoko's relationship: \\"I once had a girl, or should I say, she once had me...\\"  They have a very complicated - and for one time, sexual - relationship.  Did they or did they not love each other?<br /><br />Reading this book is like being transported to an other-wordly, ethereal place as if you are in somewhere magical.  His writing is just so beautiful and poetic, the words flow nicely on the page.  Toru reminds me of Daria.  He just goes with what life throws at him.  He doesn't think he's any special and he knows that he's flawed and accepts it.  The subject of suicide is heavily featured in this book.  In the news, I have read Japanese and Korean and other Asian young people committing suicide.  Some of them are famous actors and models.  Even though this was first published in the late 80s, it clearly is still relevant today.<br /><br />Kudos also to translator Jay Rubin who managed to maintain the integrity and intensity of the work.  Being bilingual myself, I know that are are some words in my language that have no exact translation in English.  The way he weaved the words set up a crystal clear atmosphere while maintaining the mood that Murakami's works are famous for.<br /><br />Overall, it is a tale of growing up.  A lyrical piece of fiction that captures the reader and does not let go.<br /><br />Rating: 5/5.<br /><br />Recommendation: Lovely imagery, deep emotions, and exceptional writing.  I wish everyone would read this book.he late 80s, it clearly is still relevant today.<br /><br />Kudos also to translator Jay Rubin who managed to maintain the integrity and intensity of the work. 0307887960|5.0|After doing this diet, I have read both really positive and really negative reviews of it. My opinion is that every diet has its positives and criticisms. Every diet (kind of like every product you ever read reviews about) has people who love it and people who hate it and would tell you to stay as far away from it as possible. All I can say is that this diet has worked for my husband and myself. Maybe it depends on each individual as to how it makes you feel and so forth. Every weight loss diet has some sort of extreme change, and though there may be criticisms of it, I figured that trying a diet would be better than staying overweight and eating as I was. Some people need a structured way of doing it rather than just \\"cutting back and exercising more\\". From August to December, I lost a little over 30 pounds and my husband lost a little over 60 pounds. We were strict with it though the occasional get-together did make us eat a few things we shouldn't have for just that meal. We saw that we didn't lose the weight that day and started back the next. I think a key thing was to drink the water required every day. I noticed that I lost more on the protein days when I drank the required amount of water and did the exercise. I've read some pretty harsh reviews and can only say that this has worked for us. I was even told by a trainer at a gym that I looked very healthy as I was losing the weight. That was a huge boost. We are now getting some of the regular foods back into our diet but being careful not to just allow everything back in just yet. It made sense that your body needs the time to adjust to your new weight because it will fight to get back to what it's used to. I think that's one reason why you feel pretty sluggish the first few days of the diet (apart from the energy from carbs being taken away). Your body is being deprived of what it's used to and it's trying to hold on to what it can. I liked that even though you were limited on what you could eat, you were not limited on how much you could eat. With such a busy schedule, it was so much easier to stock the fridge with allowable foods and get what you want when you want. People need to find what works for them and different people do better on different diets. Looking back, I wish that I had kept a journal during the weight loss as well as a log of the weight loss to look back and see the process. For me, this was a doable diet in which I got results and I'm feeling healthier and happier.

I am happy to feel good about being woman with a family, not just a mom and a wife. Its harder because sometimes during tantrums you just want to give in and let them have whatever they want, especially if you are in a public place and getting nasty looks. But 99% of the time I am able to stay strong. I remind myself that when she is throwing a fit, she may want a snack or a new toy. But what she needs is to learn patience and good manners. If I don't teach her, how will she learn? If she doesn't learn by practice, then how?<br /><br />I won't say my daughter is the best behaved child of all time. She goes to a daycare which is a lot like a creche in some ways, but that is where the similarities end. There is only one teacher per 7-8 children. Not nearly enough to prevent all the biting, hitting, kicking, etc that goes on among toddlers. My daughter has learned some pretty awful words and aggressive behaviors from other kids that she would never have learned in our home. This is natural and would have happened eventually once she reached school age, but I was not ready for this from a toddler. Luckily at home we are able to stop these behaviors quickly.<br /><br />She has a lot of toys, thanks to her relatives. She doesn't play with mostof them because she prefers \\"real\\" things and we believe in unstructured play. She uses her toys in creative ways I would never have thought of. We try to only give her toys which will encourage creativity. We also like to take her places that she can explore on her own. Out to fields, to museums, parks, nature paths, etc. Not just places that are themed for children. We like to just let her play on her own as much as she wants. When she wants us to play with her she comes to us and asks, but we stay out of her way until she asks us to join her. Her favorite thing to do is read. She likes us to read to her and she also likes to \\"read\\" to her animals and dolls. She basically turns the pages and babbles and points to the pictures. Thanks to teaching her to be patient she is happy to sit still and read several books at one time. Her favorite book is almost 70 pages long. She also likes to \\"give speeches\\" where she lines up her toys and she stands in front of them and talks to them and gestures wildly like she's giving a passionate speech about something super important. I have no idea where she learned that. Her pediatrician says her vocabulary, behaviors, and level of self control is far beyond her age group. I don't know how much of that is due to my parenting or what but I'm proud.<br /><br />As a result of using BUB and FKEE to drastically alter our eating habits, my daughter eats a ton of different foods that most of the kids I know would gag on. Her favorite foods include black olives and raw broccoli. She also is more well behaved in restaurants than she is at home. She sits and waits for her food, and she at least tries everything on her plate. She doesn't always eat everything but she doesn't throw her food (anymore...) and she doesn't have fits at the table. She sits still and talks to me and her dad while we finish eating and pay. At home she gets a little restless since she knows her toys are in another room and she wants to get up from the table and go play instead of waiting for me and my husband to finish eating. She hasn't learned to politely excuse herself from the meal yet, so until she does we make her sit with us until everyone has finished.<br /><br />We get compliments all the time from people in public who see her on her best behavior. Some of her first words were please and thank you. She is friendly and says hello and goodbye to everyone we meet. I can pretty much take her anywhere with me without any problems.<br /><br />BUB is perfect for me because I don't believe in spanking. I don't think there is any reason to spank ever. I give my child the tools she needs to learn to behave herself. Spanking teaches nothing except to do what the parent says in order to not get spankings. I want to teach her to have good manners and follow instructions because its the right thing to do. I feel that I have the ability to teach her right from wrong, and the reasons why she should behave a certain way. I feel that she is intelligent enough to understand these concepts. Spanking her would represent a lack of confidence in my ability to teach her and in her ability to learn. With the things I've learned in BUB, I won't ever feel like I need to spank her. I do believe in short time outs and in taking away privileges and things, because those are real consequences and they have meanings behind them. Spanking is just senseless violence and teaches nothing but more violence in the place of knowledge. So far we've never spanked and we don't think she is old enough for time out, and we also haven't needed it. She knows what's allowed and what's not. Toys that are kicked, thrown, or otherwise abused are temporarily taken away. Food that is thrown is taken away. When crayons are used to color things that are not paper, they are taken away. When she demonstrates the ability to follow rules and control herself again, she has earned back whatever was taken away. Instead it's the kind of ambiguity that forces you do to the sad calculations about all the other things you might have done with your time had you not been watching this.<br /><br />Blue-collar Ohio resident Curtis has a decent job, a nice enough house, a pretty red-haired wife, a special needs (hearing-impaired) daughter...and a dog. In response, first he makes dog live outside, and then decides he MUST upgrade his existing storm shelter...by adding a 40-foot ISO shipping container. After wetting the bed during one dream, he goes to see his doctor (who is oh-so-coincidentally familiar with his mom's case) to discuss his symptoms. And even though it's not too hard for him to arrange a $6000 home improvement loan and wrangle company equipment to bury the shipping container in his back yard, it is WAY too hard for him to make the two hour drive to Columbus to see the psychiatrist his warm and fuzzy family doctor recommends...until way too late in the unraveling of his life.<br /><br />Oh yeah: we also learn his employer's generous health plan will pay for implants needed to restore his daughter's hearing...and it will only take six weeks to get the surgery scheduled! Director Jeff Nichols should have just put up a big flashing neon said that said: \\"Curtis is about to get fired, lose his insurance and really agitate his wife.\\" Of course he does get fired (for using the company equipment at home), and from here the movie proceeds into its climax and denouement, when Curtis has an epic meltdown at a Lions' Club dinner, followed by a midwest weather event that places the whole family first in the shelter and then at the beach to confront his --and their-- fears about actual and metaphoric storms.<br /><br />Here's the thing: most of the characters are likeable enough and the acting is good enough that this movie could have been turned into an engaging story about something...if it could have ever figured out what that something is. 0307949486|3.0|Let me start by giving a bit of a disclaimer: I rarely read fiction, at least contemporary fiction. Modern fiction tends to be very formulaic within its specific genre, and there are simply too many mediocre novels out there to sort through. After hearing the great notices though, I elected to make an exception for \\"The Girl With The Dragon Tattoo,\\" Stieg Larsson's blockbuster bestseller set in small town Sweden. I had read over and over what a page turner the book was, but I have to admit despite Larsson's obvious talent, the book is slow, particularly the first 200 pages or so. I don't know whether translation issues were major factors here, but the book clearly assumes some understanding of Swedish culture, and some names and terms quickly became confusing and distracting. The first (but not only) prominent example is the use of the word \\"gaol.\\" It is clearly a jail of some sort, but does it have a specialized meaning as a certain type of institution? If so it should be translated better, if not it should be translated as \\"jail,\\" which is how my dictionary simply defined it.<br /><br />While the pacing is often ponderous, it really seemed to need editing. An astonishing amount of minutiae is included in the book, and much could have been excised to make the book move faster and flow better. Sometimes authors skillfully draw out intertwined plot elements to heighten suspense, and while Larsson does that to some extent later in the book, much of the text reads as simple trivia that's more in the way of the plot than complementing it. The story concerns Mikael Blomkvist, a disgraced journalist convicted of libel and his unlikely alliance with the emotionally bankrupt and antisocial Lisbeth Salander, the tattooed girl of the title. The book is in large part a character study: unfortunately I found the character of Lisbeth to be more interesting but far less detailed than Mikael. Lisbeth has a credibility-straining gift with computers but no ability for normal human interactions, while Mikael came across as needlessly promiscuous, unlikeable, and cold, making this a book with characters that were somewhat interesting, but that I couldn't really care about. I found some of the plot elements utterly predictable and wooden. It was, for instance, a given that the two leads would eventually have a romantic relationship, yet it when it finally happens it's as ugly, impersonal, and forced as you could imagine.<br /><br />Other plot elements bothered me: it's not impossible to hire a disgraced reporter to undertake a cold case investigation from 1966, but is that really a more reasonable choice than hiring an actual detective with law enforcement experience? The novel depends on your thinking so. That this pursuit takes the book into very unseemly dark regions of the human psyche is beyond doubt, and I found some of the details needlessly gruesome. (I particularly dislike violence directed toward children or animals.) The book is unfocused in the sense that it has both the murder investigation and evil corporation plots working around each other, and they are not as complimentary as I expected them to be. In this case the homicide is much more gripping material, while the corporate villainy angle simply serves as a distracting background to provide motivation to the characters.<br /><br />In the end, the book is a modest example of contemporary fiction, though substantially less satisfying than I expected. There is no question that Larsson was a very smart writer with a good grasp of character and general plot outline, but the extraordinary amount of exposition about trivialities, the extremely trying religious components, and the forced feeling associated with the confluence of Mikael and Lisbeth, made me frankly have a hard time caring much about the protagonists and their tribulations. The book is somewhat turgidly paced, but on balance is conceptually still at least average for contemporary popular fiction, though I didn't find it to be especially distinguished from thousands of other novels vying for your attention.o be especially distinguished from thousands of other novels vying for your attention.

B003Y5HWMW|5.0|What follows is a review of the Game of Thrones: Season One Blu ray set ... not a review of the show itself, of the series, of fantasy as a genre, or anything else.  This is a review to let you know what you're getting when you open the box and put the discs in your blu ray player.<br /><br />What you get:<br /><br />Game of Thrones comes packaged in an attractive fold-out cardboard case with a decorative slipcover.  The box art contains images of various characters in the series: a Stark, some Lannisters, a few Baratheons, and even a Snow just for good measure.  There are five discs in this set, each one emblazoned with the sigil of one of the powerful noble houses inhabiting the world of Westeros.  A handy insert is included, along with some advertisements for other GoT merchandise, providing a brief synopsis of each episode as well as its writing credits.  The insert also contains genealogies of the four main families featured in season one, as well as the members of their housholds, the royal court, and some allies.  Open up this insert, opposite all of the information previously listed, is a full map of Westeros.<br /><br />The Discs:<br /><br />The episodes are spread across all five discs which is convenient enough, but I would have preferred a separate \\"bonus\\" disc.  The special features are listed in the description, but the short version goes like this; there are commentaries on selected episodes and a handful of bonus features on each disc giving you information about houses, characters, history, locations, some making of featurettes and interviews etc.  It's all interesting stuff but nothing wildly unexpected.<br /><br />The interface itself on these discs isn't all that great, although it does look nice enough.  There is no scene selection menu which seems like a glaring omission, and the on-screen bonus features that play while you're watching an episode actually seem pretty distracting.  Sadly a lot of the \\"bonus\\" information is presented entirely astext, which is pretty crummy considering these are blu rays.  It would've been nice to see HBO shill out the extra cash to have a \\"Maester\\" conduct \\"Lessons\\" on the various houses, nations, and other powers at play in Westeros.  This feels like a missed opportunity, but I'm really picking nits.<br /><br />Load times ... oh man load times.  It takes two minutes to load the disc from selecting play on the blu ray player menu to the start of an actual episode.  This.  Is.  Bonkers.  I'm not sure what's at fault here, but I've tried this set on three different blu ray players (Sony BDP S380, Samsung BD-D6700, and an original Sony PS3 Fat/the one that's backwards compatible) and in each case the discs take about two minutes to load.  I've tried disabling BD live, but all that seems to do is make the loading icon (a moving image of a weirwood) disappear ... then again this loading icon only shows up occasionally anyway.  I've encountered reports on a few forums that in some cases people can't get their discs to load at all, fortunately I haven't had that problem and your individual mileage may very - but something is definitely up with these discs.<br /><br />Audio/Video quality:<br /><br />Visually I have no complaints with GoT.  It's honestly one of the most beautiful blu rays I've come across.  There are no artifacts of any sort visible, and black levels and fine detail are revelatory.  Textures pop with insane detail; the hair and pores on an actors face, embroidery on fabric and embossing on leather, wood grain ... it goes on and on.  There's not a single shot that isn't downright beautiful.  Even the darkest night scenes featuring the black brothers, which is essentially black on black, offer no crush or loss of fine detail ... as long as your display is up to the task.  This is one of the best releases on the blu ray format, truly perfect visual representation.<br /><br />As for the sound, uh it's good.  I guess.  I'm not an audiophile, and as such not nearly as concerned with audio fidelity as I am with the visual.  Despite that though the encode on these episodes seems nice enough.  A surround system gives a great sense of presence in all of the environments without ever getting too bass-y or blowing out the high end.  When the Hand has his first council in King's Landing there's a great sense of directionality to the conversation (voices reverberating from walls and columns).  It's one of those small details, but it really sells the experience.  All in all it's good.  And in case anyone is wondering if you're watching these episodes through headphones, a soundbar, or built-in speakers you're not missing out on anything ... I promise!<br /><br />Pros -<br />Game of Thrones: Season One on blu ray is worth the price of admission purely for the quality of the visuals alone.  All of the other stuff is just gravy.  I haven't worked my way through all of the bonus features, but they seem entertaining/educational enough so far.<br /><br />Cons -<br />The biggest one hands down is load time.  I've never come across discs that take two minutes to load up and sadly it's unlikely that anything is going to improve this built-in flaw.  Each blu ray player manufacturer will have to release a firmware update (for specific players in many cases) to address this issue which ... seems unlikely.  If you buy this set and can't stand the load times or can't get your discs to play at all - contact HBO.  Let them know that they're BD java programming sucks.<br /><br />The bonus features could be implemented a little better.  I would've like to have had commentaries on every episode, and I would have preferred a fixed commentator cast as opposed to an ever changing one.  Still what's there in the commentaries is entertaining enough, but the set feels kind of incomplete.  I don't like reading text on my television, not for any decent stretch of time.  HBO should really have taken it upon themselves to film something to show us this information, instead of copy-pasting faq-type material.  A disappointment.<br /><br />Give me scene search ... seriously this has been available on pretty much every title I've encountered since blu ray launched.  This omission is silly.<br /><br />The Verdict:<br />Despite some serious flaws in execution - load times load times - Game of Thrones is a must buy.  If you've read the books, like fantasy or genre fiction, or whatever ... you have to buy this series.  If epic fantasy isn't your cup of tea, well - borrow these from a friend then.  You just might get hooked.<br /><br />_________________________<br />Edit:<br /><br />Special Features<br />Well it hasn't taken me long to flip flop on some of my feelings already. 006197806X|4.0|Of all the books I read in 2011 (and I read around 140 according to my Goodreads stats) Delirium by Lauren Oliver is in the top three. I loved it that much. I didn't read it until last September (read my review) which is probably a good thing because the wait from September to February was LONG enough people. So, Pandemonium was obviously a pre-order for me. And yes, the wait to receive it from B&N online was excruciating as well. But it was SO worth it! Are you worried that Pandemonium will suffer the dreaded second- book- in- a series- slump? Worry not, dear friends, this book, while very different in tone than Delirium, will suck you in with it's awesome story, complex characters, heart stopping action, and indescribably beautiful writing that is the hallmark of author Lauren Oliver.<br /><br />Pandemonium begins right where Delirium leaves off. Lena has escaped into the Wilds, and in a way she never imagined: without Alex. The book features alternating chapters in time, \\"then\\" and \\"now\\". I found both of these corresponding story lines to be engaging, and it never really bothered me switching back and forth while I was reading. At a certain point in the story, the chapters seems to echo each other, and eventually converge into the \\"now.\\" I thought Oliver did a really great job with this, I was never lost or confused at any point.<br /><br />But what really grabbed me, right from the start, was how different Lena has become. I mean, it was almost like reading about an entirely different character. In Delirium, Lena starts out as a naive, frightened girl, blind to the world she is living in. She's a \\"zombie\\", as the Invalids like to call them, fully indoctrinated into the world created by the Consortium and those who want to erase all powerful emotions from the people. I loved watching Lena \\"wake up\\" and open her eyes to what was going on in Delirium. But that Lena is nothing like the Lena we encounter in Pandemonium. After struggling in the Wilds she is finally found, close to death, and taken in by a group of Invalids. As she struggles to fit in and find a way to live this new life she's been given, she also struggles with her grief of losing Alex. And her grief is palpable, described in a way that only Lauren Oliver could:<br /><br />'Grief is like sinking, like being buried. I am in water the tawny color of kicked-up dirt. Every breath is full of choking. There is nothing to hold on to, no sides, no way to draw myself up. There is nothing to do but let go.<br /><br />Let go. Feel the weight all around you, feel the squeezing of your lungs, the slow, low pressure. Let yourself go deeper, there is nothing but bottom. There is nothing but the taste of metal, and the echoes of old things, and days that look like darkness.'<br /><br />It's the grief that Lena experiences that shapes and molds her into the person she becomes. Fierce. Hard. Strong. Bitter. The way she functions and works through her loss is to become a cold, fearless, totally kick -butt warrior.For anyone that felt the pacing of Delirium was too slow, or certain pasrts were too drawn out, I really think you are going to like Pandemonium. It's very fluid, moving ever forward, and there is a ton of action scenes. The Lena in Delirium may have been an athlete, but she could have never handles what the Lena in Pandemonium handles, it's pretty awe-inspiring.<br /><br />I loved the action, but I also really loved watching the story's development. I wanted to know more about how this society works, and more about the people who put the laws in place. In Pandemonium we are introduced to the DFA (Deliria Free America), a group headed up by some pretty extreme people. I wanted to learn more about the Invalids and the Resistance, and in Pandemonium we witness the beginnings of the uprising. And I wanted to know more about Lena's mother, where she was, and what really happened to her. Some of these things are touched on briefly, but I know we are going to learn even more inthe final installment of the series. Watching Lena begin to uncover the corruption as various plot threads are twisted together is one of my favorite parts of the book.<br /><br />So what of the romance? Well, we have us a little love triangle going on here. Even though Alex is gone, he is still ever present in Lena's heart and mind. This becomes a problem when Lena meets and forms an attachment to the DFA's poster child, Julian Fineman. I will say that I admire how Lena is genuinely guilt- ridden and distressed when she begins having feelings for Julian. She is constantly comparing him to Alex, and I really liked that Lauren Oliver didn't write off those deep feelings that Lena and Alex shared in Delirium. You could feel her struggle and that made it more authentic. At the same time, liking Lena as I do, I wanted her to be able to open her heart to someone again. She had made herself so hard and cold and distant. When Julian is able to melt away some of that coldness, I though it was a wonderful thing, and I felt happy for them both. No, he's not Alex, but Julian is not without merit. He is just as blind as Lena once was, but like Lena in Delirium, when the chance at love comes his way, he takes it.<br /><br />'Julian steps out of the shadows. Keeping one arm wrapped around my chest, I grab for my shirt.<br />\\"Wait,\\" he calls out, and something about the tone of his voice--a note of command, and also of urgency--stops me.<br />\\"Wait,\\" he repeats, more softly. We're separated by twenty feet of space, but the way he's looking at me makes me feel as though we we're chest to chest, I can feel his eyes on my skin like a prickling touch. I know I should put on my shirt, but I can't move. I can hardly even breathe.<br />\\"I've never been able to look before,\\" Julian says simply, and takes one more step toward me. The light falls differently on his face, and now I can see a softness in his eyes, a blur, and it makes the roaring heat in my body melt away into warmth, a steady, wonderful feeling. At that same time, a tiny voice in the back of my head pipes up: Danger, danger, danger. Beneath it, a fainter echo: Alex, Alex, Alex.<br />Alex used to look at me like that.<br />\\"Your waist is so small.\\" That's all Julian says: in a voice so quiet I barely hear him.'<br /><br />It's really a lovely encounter between them, and in it you can see the seeds of something beginning to blossom. How can you not cheer for that, even just a little?<br /><br />And again, we have the treat of witnessing Lauren Oliver's incredible way with the written word. I swear that woman could write tax laws and I would gobble them up and ask for second helpings.<br /><br />'I was not prepared for the feeling of the sunshine, or the smell of growth and life. All around us are enormous trees, leaves just tinged with yellow as though they are catching fire slowly from the outside, patterning the ground with alternating spots of light and shadow. For a second something deep and old rises inside me and I could fall on the ground and weep for joy, or open up my arms and spin.'<br /><br />Just so beautiful. I'm speechless and completely in awe of her talent.<br /><br />So by now you know from the pre-release and post release buzz that there is another cliffhanger. Honestly, it was no big shock, I saw it coming (I think anyone who reads this will probably see it coming) and yes, it ripped me at my heart as I read the last lines of the book. Just enough to make me rack my brains and wonder who I can pay off to get an early copy of Requiem. Any takers? Just kidding (but not really.)<br /><br />Overall I really, really enjoyed Pandemonium. The only reason(s) it didn't get a full 5 stars from me was because 1)I miss Hana and Grace, and yes, even Aunt Carol a little. I hope we see more of them in Requiem, I guess I need to read the novella Hana to make up for their absence in Pandemonium. and 2) while I did think the Lena and Julian romance was sweet, and fitting for this stageof the story, I miss the swoon factor that was Lena and Alex. I can't help it, I'm a romantic by nature.<br /><br />4.5/5 Stars<br /><br />Cover Thoughts<br /><br />Like the reissue of Delirium last year, the cover of Pandemonium features the same model among more botanical goodness, but this one is in shades of reds, and oranges. B000THCKF2|5.0|A Passion Play is quite possibly the most under-appreciated work by Jethro Tull.  I will grant you that in the beginning it is difficult to get into but if you give it a chance, as a whole the album is a work of genius.<br /> I do find amusement in those that rail about \\"The Hare who lost his spectacles\\"  In order to understand the purpose of that interlude you may want to understand exactly what a passion play is.  The hare who lost his spectacles is silly, filled with goofy word puns and slighty off pronunciations.  It is a parable and an interlude between the seriousness of a mans journey into the afterlife and the resulting judgement of his life.  The silliness is an intentional break between the very heavy subject matter that A Passion Play revolves around.<br /> The music, especially the instrumental passages, is fantastic and vary from hard rock to classical to Spanish flamenco guitar.  It amazes me how well Ian Anderson and Jethro Tull as a whole blends such a variety of sounds and styles so smoothly.  The transitions are nothing short of brilliant. Add in the heavily philosophical and oft satirical lyrics and you have one incredible piece of music.  If youre looking for pop, look elsewhere, you will be disappointed.  If youre looking for something deeper, than that, then this just may be what youre looking for.  You owe it to yourself to find out.

B007K3JCAE|5.0|While the movie is certainly worthy of praise and is a very excellent sequel to the first one, my review will be focusing on what now has become a monumental case of false advertising about getting a \\"Digital Copy\\" product. To be fair to the movie, I did give this a 5 star rating. There are other excellent descriptions of the movie already published under reviews so I am focusing on the product.<br /><br />What you are getting with this and any other \\"Ultraviolet\\" product is NOT a digital copy. You don't get a copy of anything. All you get is the right to create an account to access the servers of the so called \\"Ultraviolet\\" system. Hence, if you are at an airport, you get the joy of using your data plan to capacity (and paying actual additional dollars if you have exceeded capacity) only to realize that while on the plane, you have no access to your purported digital copy. This is one of the biggest scams in the digital age. Advertising to let you think that you are getting a \\"copy\\" of a movie, when all you get is access to a web site, when it is up, using your own internet bandwidth and the dollars that go along with it. If consumers don't fight this severe advertising deception, soon true digital copies will be a thing of the past and you will be stuck with Bluray vs. iTunes. Buyer beware. Stand up to Ultraviolet and don't let them ruin the digital copy experience. We are all paying good money for these. The least we could get in return is a digital copy to take with us, legally on a plane ride or traveling otherwise.<br /><br />Again, remember, all you get is access to the Ultraviolet website with no guarantee of its continued existence. That is not getting a copy of anything at all. Just a promise that as of today, you will be able to access a web site owned by a greedy conglomerate of movie studios who want to prevent you from having a portable copy of the movie so they don't have to share any profit with companies like Apple.

Where people once owned cars for pleasure and convenience, for some, their car may be their only remaining possession.<br /><br />Charles Howard (Bridges) is a man who started with 21 cents in his pocket and turned it into what most would consider a successful business. B004ASNG04|5.0|This book tracks the development of human civilisation from around 10000BC to the modern day. It confronts many of the myths of history that has plagued comparisons between the West and the East. The book takes time to define the concept of Eastern and Western cores and tracks their development and shifting locations. Ultimately it seeks to answer the question as to why does the Western core seems to be on top for now. The general impression from the book is that the West rule through a combination of geographic luck and a slightly out of sync development cycle with the Eastern core. Overall a fantastic book and well worth the read. It will confront some misconceptions about the differences between East and West and give the reader a better appreciation for the world we live in today.

0517548232|5.0|Henry Hazlitt was not only a gifted economist. He wrote in a no-nonsense style, demolishing seemingly persuasive, highly complex or even tortured arguments in favour of suboptimal economic policies. Much as Copernicus placed the sun, rather than the earth, at the centre of the solar system, thereby greatly simplifying astronomical calculation and eventually enabling Kepler and Newton to explain planetary motion and gravity in simple, eloquent equations, so Hazlitt masterfully simplifies the world of economics, in both theory and practice. Each chapter of Economics in One Lesson is a tour-de-force critique of one or more misguided economic policies. In his own time, using his powerful economic insight, he was able to predict a broad range of future economic developments, including the dollar devaluation and stagflation of the 1970s, the devastating impact of rent control on urban housing, the counterproductive effects of trade barriers and the distorting aspects of many forms of regulation, taxation and government subsidies. Armed with the common sense knowledge explained cogently in this volume, non-economist readers will find they can make much better sense of economic developments and see far easier through politicians' and policy makers' frequently deceptive arguments and false promises. Economics in One Lesson is more than just an outstanding book, it is probably the best introductory book on economics ever written.<br />John Butler, author of The Golden Revolution: How to Prepare for the Coming Global Gold Standard (John Wiley and Sons, 2012)
B005MJFA2W|5.0|This book will illuminate the reader to how the brain works through its two different aspects. The author presents the view that there's a slow brain and a fast brain. The slow brain is the one you control, it's methodical, calculating and generally the one in charge. The fast brain is the one that lets you make snap decisions and generally drives a lot of the things we do each day on auto pilot. The author takes time to present many examples that illustrate the two sides of the brain as well as covering many other psychological topics such as regression to the mean etc. Overall an excellent read and for anyone who has an interest in how and why we make the decisions we do then they will find this book a very worthwhile read.

0140177191|5.0|The late Marion Zimmer Bradley's achievement with The Mists of Avalon is remarkable. She broke new ground by completely re-imagining the Arthurian legends, a Herculean literary task. Modern successors include Wicked--now a hit musical--and various examples of female heroines replacing male heroes. Yes, there is a nebulous 'feminist' aspect to this book, but really I would characterise it as 'humanist' or 'individualist' instead. The heroes of this book, male or female, are those that think for themselves, rather than for someone or something else. Reason, experience and a willingness to listen to Mother Nature: These qualities enable the heroes of the book to triumph, at least to some extent, against incipient tyranny. Yet there is no real redemption here. Fans of Norse mythology and its relative lack of any redemption will enjoy this epic. Indeed, I would go so far as to claim that Ms Bradley is re-imagining the Norse sagas as much or more as the overt Arthurian plot that gives the book its core structure. Nevertheless, this book is far too rich and complex to be summarised properly in a review of this length. I recommend it, to be sure.<br />In the interest of full disclosure, now in my 40s, I read this book as a teenager and, you might say, it changed my life. For the better I might add. 1451648537|5.0|You're either an Apple Fan boy or you're not (I'm not, as a rule). I suspect many of the reviews of this book are, therefore, proxy votes not on Walter Isaacson's talent, but Steve Jobs'.<br /><br />Isaacson has, as the heart of his book, the Steve Jobs business philosophy. It is this, as much as jobs' unnatural flair for design, which separates him from his peers: most of the rest of the founding fathers and prophets of the tech revolution - Hewlett, Packard, Grove, Gates, Brin and Page, Fanning, Lessig, Zitrain - even Apple's own Wozniak - are united by the same insight: that modular components which put the decision in the hands of the user make for a tremendously powerful thing. This is the genuinely revolutionary, anarchic disposition which has shocked the political, corporate and social order over the last 20 years. It's a revolution of which Steve Jobs, and his beloved Apple, wanted no part.<br /><br />It is difficult to conceive of how different the world now is - hard to remember how rigid and immovable was the social architecture which the internet blew away (but which, if Zitrain is to be believed are regrouping forces). Flying in the face of the Web 2.0 dogma, Steve Jobs, virtually alone, believed in closed, tightly integrated, designed, controlled products. This remains a profoundly old-fashioned outlook for a tech company to adopt.<br /><br />Isaacson does an excellent job of highlighting a colossal *conceptual* chasm between Apple and the rest that most users simply won't credit. An iPhone is, philosophically a very different thing to an android smartphone. An iMac is a profoundly different thing to a PC. Your preference between them still comes down to an instinct: as between closed and open; tight and loose; predictable and flexible; probable and possible; centrally planned and laissez faire; design and fiat and even, melodramatically, God and evolution. These days most folks seem to prefer centrally planned, at any rate inasmuch as they buy Apple's articulation of it.<br /><br />Apple's articulation of it, of course, has been incredible. Even Bill Gates acknowledges that Apple has made some really cool stuff. (I'm hacking this review out on a dented Blackberry Curve, but coveting the iPad on the lap next to me. It irks me, but I really want one). And Steve Jobs accomplished this in a way that illustrates the validity of his model, however awkward that might be for the political orthodoxy. And he was a beastly bit of work while he was at it.<br /><br />Walter Isaacson gives the most sullen Googlista reason to respect Steve Jobs' achievement, and the most fawning mac apologist reason to abhor him. Jobs was a difficult individual with little of the grace and style of his products. He was prone to angry tirades, and was often instantly dismissive of what turned out to be excellent suggestions. He was similar with his family. Those who stayed close enough, personally or professionally, to understand how he ticked (notably Apple co-founder Steve Wozniak, wife Laurene Powell, chief Apple designer Jony Ive and Isaacson himself) learned to accommodate his foibles remarkably effectively, ignored the histrionics and did what they felt was the right thing anyway. Which, as Isaacson makes clear, is what Jobs expected anyway.<br /><br />This is a well told story about the singular vision of a remarkable man.<br /><br />Olly Buxtonarticulation of it.<br /><br />Apple's articulation of it, of course, has been incredible. 1400069289|5.0|\\"The Power Of Habit: Why We Do What We Do And How To Change It\\" by Charles Duhigg is a fascinating look at how habits work with very interesting case studies and research.  According to Duhigg, the key to becoming more productive, exercising regularly, tapping into our reserves of creativity, building revolutionary companies and social movements, conquering our most stubborn vices, and, ultimately, achieving success is to understand how habits work.  After reading this book, you will understand just how powerful habits really are, and realize the truth behind Duhigg's statement.<br /><br />The case studies Duhigg uses to illustrate how powerful habits can be are engaging and enlightening.  They made me stop and think about things I've done, and how I'd been influenced, and how maybe some of my habits were influenced by outside forces.  It was also a little shocking to see how habits are related to what some may call addiction, most notably in the study on a woman who's gambling habit spiraled out of control.<br /><br />The book is divided into three main parts.  The first, The Habits of Individuals, contains chapters on the habit loop, how habits work; the craving brain, how to create new habits; and the golden rule of habit change, why transformation occurs.  Part two, The Habits of Successful Organizations, starts with a chapter on keystone habits, or the Ballad of Paul O'Neill, which habits matter most; then goes to Starbucks and the habit of success, when willpower becomes automatic; the power of a crisis, how leaders create habits through accident and design; and how Target knows what you want before you do, when companies predict (and manipulate) habits.  Part three, The Habit of Societies has two chapters, and these focus on Saddleback Church and the Montgomery Bus Boycott, how movements happen; and the neurology of free will, are we responsible for our habits?<br /><br />I really think this book will open the eyes of many people.  Especially enlightening is how large corporations analyze buying habits and patterns to specifically market toward those habits.  Learning how stores such as Target collect, analyze, and use information was extremely interesting if not a bit scary when you think about what organizations know about people, and how they influence what people buy and use.<br /><br />The book is a fun and interesting read, but also very educational.  The knowledge gleaned from this text has the power to help individuals as well as businesses.  There is an Appendix titled \\"A Reader's Guide to Using These Ideas.\\"  Unfortunately, this Appendix was not included in the Advanced Reader's Edition I'm reviewing, so I can't comment on how clearly and easily the author explains how to use the information.  With that said, a little thinking on the concepts contained in this book will allow any reader to incorporate ideas in a variety of ways.  However, it might not be easy or quick.  The author points out, \\"Transforming a habit, or a life, isn't necessarily easy or quick.  It isn't always simple.  But it is possible.\\"  I highly recommend this book to anyone who wants to learn about habits and explore the possibilities of creating or transforming habits.  They really are powerful.<br /><br />Reviewed by Alain Burrese, J.D., author of the \\"Tough Guy Wisdom\\" series and others.how large corporations analyze buying habits and patterns to specifically market toward those habits. We've all been waiting for \\"The Thing 2\\" for a long time, and this film delivers.<br /><br />  The producers deserve credit for being so faithful to the John Carpenter movie.  Often sequels and prequels are decried by fans because the writers go off in \\"new directions\\".  (Look what happened to \\"Doom\\" and the \\"Highlander\\" sequels, for example.)<br /><br />  No, for this movie they studied the Norwegian camp as it was shown in the Carpenter film, and meticulously depicted how every little detail came to be -- right down to the axe in the wall!  And they made the movie end right where the previous one starts.<br /><br />  While the film has parallels to the 1982 movie, it adds new material as well.  The researchers discover more about what the Thing can and can't do, and they see the alien spacecraft in more detail.<br /><br />  Many reviewers complained that there was too much CGI in the film.  If you watch some of the \\"Making of\\" bonus material, you'll see that there was not as much CGI as you may think.  The creatures' bodies were made of good ol' foam rubber and puppetry.  CGI was only used to create difficult limb movements, tentacles, and transformations.  The \\"problem\\" was that the puppetry was so good, it was hard to tell where it ended and the CGI began.<br /><br />  The one problem this movie could not surmount is that they didn't have Kurt Russell.  Mary Winstead does her best in the leading role, but she lacks the toughness of a Sigourney Weaver.  Hey, not everyone can pull off a convincing fight against infectious aliens in an enclosed space.<br /><br />  The Thing 2011 is faithful to the 1982 movie, but also holds new surprises.  If you're a fan of the Carpenter flick, you should definitely see this.

B00536OCYG|5.0|I've owned Pink Floyd \\"The Wall\\" on cassette, early vinyl, CD, and now this heavy vinyl reissue.  It is my favorite rock album overall.  I remember purchasing this record on cassette when it first came out.  After listening to it all the way through for the first time in 1980, it was clear to me that it was and is an extraordinary classic, arguably PF's greatest album.  This is PF's most fully realized album with so much happening both musically and lyrically that it challenges you to listen and understand the powerful concept and all of the tragedy that feeds into it.  I remember being 16 years old when it was first released and digging into the lyrics to understand their message.  It was then and some 30 odd years later it still is an emotionally powerful album.  \\"The Wall\\" just doesn't succeed as a rock album; it succeeds as a true work of art.  It is like a Shakespeare tragedy that's not designed to make us happy, but to help us learn from life's sufferings on both an historical level and on a personal level.  Don't we all build walls in our lives in some way?  The message of \\"The Wall\\" is to break down the walls that prevent us from being truly happy people.<br /><br />You're probably more interested in the reissue rather than my musings about the album's art.  It's a very nice 180 gram pressing that was remastered by James Guthrie, who was one of the original people who worked on \\"The Wall\\".  The source tapes were used.  The remaster is excellent, and it sounds good right out of the jacket.  I cleaned the visually perfect records with a VPI wet/vac RCM, and the sound was illuminated after the cleaning.  I played the records with my classic SONY STR-V5 85 watts/channel clean power receiver with a Technics turntable equipped with a GRADO cartridge through a pair of NHT Classic Three book shelf speakers on stands.  I used the flat response settings.  The sound is rich and full, yet it sounds balanced.  I did encounter some noise on Side 3 in some spots, but I'm thinking further RCM cleanings will reduce or eliminate the noise.<br /><br />The packaging is very nice.  The jacket has much of the original artwork and some new artwork on the back.  It comes with a big 32\\"x38\\" poster that has all of the album's lyrics with the white brick wall in the background.  You also receive a card with a code to complete a free MP3 download of the entire album.  You get pink poly inner sleeves as well as the original paper sleeves (heavier than the original sleeves) with the lyrics on them that came with the original vinyl release.<br /><br />Overall this is an excellent reissue.  Some have complained about the price.  Great music will command a higher price, and we're talking about one of the greatest rock albums of all time.  Whether or not what's in this package is worth it to you will depend on your own personal standards.  If this album means as much to you as it does to me, you will have to have it, and remember, \\"...the ones who really love you walk up and down outside the wall...\\".

0374275637|5.0|What a treasure this book is! It's not only a masterfully written, highly accessible, and fascinating summary of Daniel Kahneman's life-time (and Nobel-prize-receiving) contributions to behavioral economics, it's also a humbling tour of both the capability--and the culpability--of our thinking.<br /><br />In the beginning of the book, Kahneman highlights the dilemma of our thinking:<br />\\"As we navigate our lives, we normally allow ourselves to be guided by impressions and feelings, and the confidence we have in our intuitive beliefs and preferences is usually justified. But not always. We are often confident even when we are wrong.\\" (p. 4)<br /><br />His aim of the book is to help us navigate this dilemma and to<br />\\"improve the ability to identify and understand errors of judgement and choice, in others and eventually in ourselves, by providing a richer and more precise language to describe them. In at least some cases, an accurate diagnosis may suggest an intervention to limit the damage that bad judgments and choices often cause.\\" (p. 4)<br /><br />This richer and more precise language begins with giving names to the two systems that drive our (fast and slow) thinking: System 1 and System 2. While System 1 operates automatically and quickly and often outside of our conscious awareness, System 2 slows the thinking process down and allows us to perform mental activities that require effort, such as complex computation and decision making. In essence, System 1 accounts for our intuitive and emotion-based thinking, and System 2 is behind our attentional, deliberate, and logical thinking. The dance between the two systems is rather remarkable, and allows us to manage the endless choices and decisions we make in our day-to-day lives. But, this dance also has quite a few missteps that result in cognitive errors and intuition biases. By giving language to label the sources of these missteps--such as anchoring effects, narrow framing, intensity matching, non-regressive predicting, and excessive coherence--we can \\"bring together in memory everything we know about a bias, its causes, its effects, and what can be done about it.\\" (p. 418)<br /><br />And, that's exactly what this book provides: A language for understanding and navigating the way we think....and the way we think about our thinking. Guiding us through prospect theory--the behavioral economics adaptation to the utility theory that is assumed in standard economics--Kahneman gives us the words to identify, describe, and manage the natural human tendency to make decisions based on perceived gains and losses, and not on actual statistics and facts. His terms of \\"remembering self\\" and \\"experiencing self\\" provide a framework for thinking about how we can maximize our well-being by avoiding the common traps which he labels as the peak-end rule, duration neglect, the focusing illusion, and miswanting. Throughout the book, Kahneman's mastery of human thinking is put into words that are beyond words. And, by the end of the book, this language comes together to beautifully tell the story of the human mind.<br /><br />My recommendation: Think fast and get your hands on a copy of this book. Then, read it slowly and take in every word of the language that will forever change the way you think about thinking.predicting, and excessive coherence--we can \\"bring together in memory everything we know about a bias, its causes, its effects, and what can be done about it.\\" (p. 418)<br /><br />And, that's exactly what this book provides: A language for understanding and navigating the way we think....and the way we think about our thinking. Guiding us through prospect theory--the behavioral economics adaptation to the utility theory that is assumed in standard economics--Kahneman gives us the words to identify, describe, and manage the natural human tendency to make decisions based on perceived gains and losses, and not on actual statistics and facts. His terms of \\"remembering self\\" and \\"experiencing self\\" provide a framework for thinking about how we can maximize our well-being by avoiding the common traps which he labels as the peak-end rule, duration neglect, the focusing illusion, and miswanting. Throughout the book, Kahneman's mastery of human thinking is put into words that are beyond words. And, by the end of the book, this language comes together to beautifully tell the story of the human mind.<br /><br />My recommendation: Think fast and get your hands on a copy of this book. Then, read it slowly and take in every word of the language that will forever change the way you think about thinking.

030788743X|3.0|I had heard great things about \\"Ready Player One\\", and I needed some escapism, so I read the whole thing today, cover to cover, to get the full experience in one sitting.<br /><br />Unfortunately, despite the rave reviews I had read elsewhere, I found \\"Ready Player One\\" to be a definite disappointment. It was, for the most part, rather shallow. The plot kept me going, but just barely -- and I repeatedly got the feeling I've read all this before, but it was done better the first time.<br /><br />Players speak with a stilted artificiality, and I feel like I'm reading a comic book or a young-adult novel most of the time through it. Every so often the main character goes off into a monologue about something, like how he believes Jesus is no more real than Santa Claus or the Easter Bunny, and he thinks adults who are religious are gullible fools -- but that's not depth of character, that's cramming the author's own views down our throats.<br /><br />Other aspects of the author's views and how he forces them on us are just as annoying, like how he makes sure we positively know the main character isn't offended when he encounters a token lesbian (don't worry, it's not important to the plot in any way), but the author also casually in a throw-off way makes sure we know the main character doesn't consider transgender women to be \\"real\\" women, as opposed to women who were born that way.<br /><br />The plot moves from point A to point B to point C, with occasional deus ex machina to get the characters out of tough spots -- much like the plot in a typical video-game adventure, and I'm sorry, but I don't consider video games to generally be great fiction. Maybe making the novel itself emulate the dull, lifeless plot of a typical video game was the author's intention, but I doubt it.<br /><br />People told me that I would love the book because I grew up as a geeky, video-gaming, roleplayer in the 80s, and I would catch all the references. And I did catch pretty much every singlereference, having spent far too many hours playing almost every game mentioned, and knowing everything from the Monty Python quotes to the Dungeon and Dragons modules to the strategies for playing the different Atari 2600 games so revered by the novel's author.<br /><br />But I didn't love \\"Ready Player One\\". Catching the references was, indeed, one of the minor \\"thrills\\" of the book, but that quickly got dull, like I was reading a trivia book about the 80s.<br /><br />FYI, in \\"Ready Player One,\\" there's a big, multi-player virtual game world called OASIS. And in case you haven't heard of it, there's already an actual virtual-reality program out there called \\"SecondLife,\\" which is a heck of a lot like OASIS in almost every important way, in that it's a huge, multi-world, multi-themed online simulation that's free to play -- and it exists for real, today. I should know, because I used to be a serious SecondLife addict, spending untold hours and days immersed in it, losing myself from the drudgery of the real world. So that idea in the book was remarkably familiar to me, and I think it's worth checking out on Google if you plan to read this book.<br /><br />Most of us probably know the story of \\"Charlie and the Chocolate Factory\\", about a reclusive genius who decides to retire but he doesn't have an heir, so he devises a series of tests, with the winner getting it all, instantly becoming the company's super-rich CEO. And a bunch of kids from different backgrounds try, but the true hero of the story is a young, innocent, incredibly poor boy with a wonderful heart.<br /><br />All that sound familiar yet? Try taking \\"Charlie and the Chocolate Factory,\\" but instead make it where the winner becomes the CEO of \\"SecondLife\\", and you get \\"Ready Player One.\\"<br /><br />Maybe I wanted more from the book showing how truly dystopian the world has become. What are things like outside of the cities, for example? Or outside of the United States? Or, most importantly, outside of thevirtual world of OASIS? Have wolves gone extinct? Do some people still live as farmers in rural Kansas? Does the Amazon rain forest still exist? How bad truly are things for the world -- and how truly important is the fate of OASIS in grand the scheme of things?<br /><br />By the end, \\"Ready Player One\\" came off to me as pointless an expenditure of time as if you just decided to play video games all day. It was fun for a while -- but now I wish I has spent the day in the sunshine enjoying the real world.<br /><br />And actually, one of the most important secondary characters in the novel would agree with me, I think. It's weird in that the entire novel seems to praise OASIS and talk about how wonderful it is, and how it must be defended by the evil people who want to take it over and turn it into a commercial nightmare. Everybody in the novel worships it practically, and couldn't imagine life without it because it's so great.<br /><br />That is, except the original partner of the man who created OASIS. The partner condemns OASIS as nightmarish in and of itself. He attacks it because of the fact it's so addictive, it has led almost everybody in the world to stop trying to fix all the problems around them, and instead gotten them to just chuck it all and escape into a false virtual reality whenever they get a chance. He blames OASIS for being one of the main reasons humanity has allowed the world to fall into a dystopia instead of actually doing something to save it all.<br /><br />You'd think that would cause a conflict in the mind of the protagonist, right? How maybe the thing he's trying to save (OASIS) isn't worth saving? Maybe it should be destroyed after all? Maybe it wouldn't be that bad if the evil people really did take it over, because then people would start logging out and looking around themselves instead of living in a perfect virtual world all the time? Maybe if OASIS wasn't free and flawless, people would rise up and protest, instead of not caring thatmajor corporations can enslave somebody for life just for falling three months behind in credit-card payments (which actually happens in the book).<br /><br />But there was no conflict about it for the main character. We're supposed to think he's awesome because he spent thousands of hours watching Silver Spoons marathons and playing Intellivision games and memorizing the lines to Wargames and Ferris Beuller's Day Off -- but we're supposed to not notice that he doesn't really care about the real world around him. And, to be honest, being a walking-talking 80s encyclopedia doesn't make for a great person, and because that's what all the main characters are in this book, I really didn't like any of them that much.<br /><br />You might get a kick out of reading \\"Ready Player One\\" if you want a light diversion for an afternoon, or if you pine for old episodes of Family Ties and Square Pegs, or if you think Duran Duran was the greatest band that ever existed. Just don't expect great literature -- or deep insights into the universe the author has created, and how it got to be the way it is.

I'd definitely encourage Spielberg and Jackson to make another Tintin movie, this time with a bit more emphasis on plot.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|I reacted the same way every old school Battle Royale fan probably did when they saw the commercials for the upcoming movie - \\"Oh great, another hack author ripping off someone else's idea, exactly what we need.\\" However upon hearing several friends swear up and down how good this book was, I decided to reserve judgement and borrow it from the library.<br /><br />To put it simply, I was dead wrong. Yes, the premise is the same as Battle Royale (children forced to fight to the death to instill fear in the populace and reassure the government of it's absolute control), however that is the only similarity. Saying they are the same would be like saying the Mentalist and Psych are the same - yes, they both have a phoney psychic who solves crimes, but as anyone who has seen both knows, they are very different shows - one is about a men hellbent on vengeance no matter what the cost, it's extremely serious and heartbreaking. The other is a light-hearted comedy.<br /><br />Frankly, the Battle Royale was a wonderful movie, a great horror show, and extremely enjoyable. But it in no way delves into the psychology in the Hunger Games. The BR folks are in a society exactly like our own except that occasionally a middle school class is selected to be killed off. But Hunger Games is set in a far off future, a wasteland where the United States once was, where people regularly starve to death and an iron government controls it's people's every move. It's far more similar to 1984 than Battle Royale (and I've read the BR book, own the movie, and owned several of the graphic novels).<br /><br />More importantly, rather than random teenagers with very little plot development who serve as mere fodder for the ensuing slaughter (as seen in BR), we are given a first person view from Katniss, the main character. Katniss takes the place of her sister in the games, something unheard of since all tributes from her district normally die in a game where 24 people fight to the death.<br /><br />This book is heart-wrenching and a real page turner. And it does all this while not taking things to the grotesque level that BR does (Hunger Games could be read by a teenager, or perhaps a pre-teen, but trust me when I say you do NOT what anyone under the age of 16 reading Battle Royale).<br /><br />Bottom line? This is a good book, and not a rip-off at all. I am definitely going to see the movie if I can scrape up the money.

1451648537|4.0|I agree with other reviewers that this biography of Steve Jobs provides a lot of insight into the life and personality of one of the most iconic and influential figures in all of modern technology.  I now do understand Jobs' mercurial nature, his bravado, powers of persuasion, quirky and sometimes abusive behavior of others in the workplace, as well as his personal vision and his genius.  For these insights the book is well worth the long read, as I like many others, are curious as to what makes a modern industrial giant like Jobs different from the rest of us.<br /><br />I did find Isaacson's style of writing, recounting parts of hundreds of interviews with dozens of Jobs' associates, competitors, collaborators and family, a bit monotonous and repetitive.  I also agree that the author is not a \\"techie\\", but a biographer, and was not able to get into much of the technical aspects of what makes Apple's products revolutionary -- except at the naive level of the consumer.  Perhaps this is sufficient for the task.  The content of the book is obviously filtered through Isaacson's perspective; no author is completely objective, so I wonder if another biographer would have brought out other details or presented the same information in a different way.<br /><br />One major omission from the book is almost no coverage of the production side of Apple.  Isaacson mentions that Steve Jobs told President Obama that Apple had 300,000 factory workers in China producing its products and that they needed to be supported by some 38,000 engineers.  I feel that the author should have included an entire chapter on what it takes for these factories to produce something like 90 million IPADs in the first year etc.  The details of the amassing of resources, the manufacture, quality control and distribution of Apple's suite of products, and the people who do it, are just as astonishing, interesting and important as the design phase which is covered in the book.<br /><br />I also wonder about the legacy of Steve Jobs.  The book says he will still be an iconic figure in 100 years, and lionized as the likes of Thomas Edison or Henry Ford.  But I wonder if technology will have advanced to such a level in another century that everything Jobs did will seem remote and trivial by that time.  He'll be remembered, of course, but not a household word like \\"Edison\\".the legacy of Steve Jobs. 1439192812|4.0|This is familiar terrain for Richard Dawkins: the crisp, rational science master patiently unmasking the silly myths and smoke and mirrors of the Religious Studies teachers. This book is an excellent introduction to science. A little dry and stuffy with its own sense of rightness in places, but the various chapters do an excellent job of deconstructing some well known (and some lesser known) myths about how the universe is constructed, and laying out the scientific reasons for how it all comes together in crisp, clear prose.<br /><br />Some of his theories regarding genes came across as slightly socially Darwinist, but this is a long running theme of his work going back to the Selfish Gene. Also, he is prepared to allow for the limitations of science so long as they don't run the risk of undermining some of his cherished anti-religion ideas. For example, in the chapter on the possibility of extra-terrestrial life he concludes that, whether it is uncovered or not, there is absolutely no chance that a creator will be revealed. Ah - there are more things in heaven and earth Horatio...<br /><br />Well worth buying for children as an introduction to science, and scientific method.

0439023521|4.0|You know a book is good when its name is much more known than the author.  I suppose that's every author's dream--to write a book that stands on its own and becomes a standard household name.  So popular, this book will be released as a movie later this week.<br /><br />\\"The Hunger Games\\" by Suzanne Collins follows Katniss Everdeen as she enters the games.  She lives in District 12, the lowest of twelve.  Every year, each district must provide two tributes: one male, one female to compete to the death.  The last one standing wins his or her district a year of increased food rations.  So all Katniss has to do is outlive twenty-three opponents.  Sound easy enough?<br /><br />The story is exciting.  The characters are all different and fun.  These two aspects alone give this book its uncommon success.  The writing itself is just average.  You won't find many quotables beyond what the characters say.  The writing mostly does a good job of staying out of the way so the reader can enjoy the story (a technique I'm good at, myself).<br /><br />Yet the book does have its slow parts.  Most notably, the first few pages.  Many incomplete sentences.  Even some character's quotes.  When I first picked up the book, I fell asleep around page 4.  If I didn't already know what a smashing success the book was, I might have stopped.  When I asked my kids and friends about it, they replied, \\"Yeah, all three books start out really slow, but then they pick up quickly.\\"  What's funny is that when the action picks up, the incomplete sentences disappear.  And when the action wanes and we see a little respite, the incomplete sentences come back en masse.  I found myself wishing Collins had done just one more round of editing to smooth some of these out and improve the flow.<br /><br />And I think back to the advice we aspiring writers are given: Grab your readers on the first page.  Perhaps this is easily overcome by an awesome story that everyone wants to read.<br /><br />Collins chooses to write in first person present, which means you see the action exactly as it's occurring from Katniss's point of view.  This also means that you don't know if she's going to die or not.  You know it's possible for the book to end this way: \\"I'm dying.  They won.  Those jerks!\\"  It's takes a while to get used to this, but then you don't notice it anymore.  The most challenging part of this technique is dealing with the passage of time, and Collins does well.<br /><br />This is a YA book, so go ahead and let your kids read it.  The book is graphic without going into every last gory detail.  The dialogue is contentious without resorting to vulgar language.  The book concentrates mainly on the story and how people react, as all good books should.<br /><br />I enjoyed reading this book (after getting past the first chapter), and I'm looking forward to the movie that comes out later this week.write in first person present, which means you see the action exactly as it's occurring from Katniss's point of view.
