0066620996|5.0|In politics, it has been said that the perfect is the enemy of the good. Author and management researcher Jim Collins turns that notion on its head and says, \\"Good is the enemy of the great.\\" The difference is this: In the political arena, the pedantic striving for the perfect legislative bill (in the eyes of its sponsors) doesn't take into account the need for compromise; thus the good can be all too easily sacrificed with nothing achieved because nothing passes the vote. Collins is not talking about compromise. He is talking about performance; and leads the reader into a detailed examination of some truly superior performing companies and what characteristics distinguish them above the merely good performers.<br /><br />Collins gives the first hint on where he's headed on pages 15-16: \\"It [the book] is not about business per se. It is ultimately about one thing: the timeless principles of good to great. It's about how you take a good organization and turn it into one that produces sustained great results, using whatever definition of results best applies to your organization.\\" Collins says the reason he used publicly traded companies to study is because the data is easily accessible and there is a widely agreed upon definition of results. But he sees it as a human problem. \\"If we have cracked the code on the question of good to great, we should have something of value to any type of organization.\\" The book itself delves into interesting details on business performance but a \\"monograph\\" accompanies the book covering the \\"social sectors.\\"<br /><br />My criticism of business books in general (as well as self-help books) is that the authors tend to recycle old ideas by creating new terminology, a separate language if you will, and make fine distinctions where there are no real differences. How else are you going to sell books if there is nothing \\"new\\" in it? Collins could be accused of some of that, e.g. \\"The Stockdale Paradox\\" and \\"The Hedgehog Concept,\\" but there are a few surprises. The biggest surprise to me was that I never heard of the CEO's from the 11 companies chosen as great out of the 1435 companies examined! The great companies selected were Abbott Laboratories, Circuit City, Fannie Mae, Gillette, Kimberly-Clark, Kroger, Nucor, Philip Morris, Pitney Bowes, Walgreen, and Wells Fargo--and there isn't a \\"charismatic\\" messianic celebrity leader among them. Empirical data showed some findings \\"inside the black box:\\"<br /><br />Ten of the eleven CEO's came from inside the company.<br />Executive compensation was not a key driver in performance.<br />All companies have long-range strategies but the great companies spent no more time on them than poorer performers.<br />Great companies focused not only on what to do but equally what not to do.<br />Technology can accelerate transformation from good to great but it cannot cause it.<br />Mergers and acquisitions played virtually no role. Two mediocre companies joining just make a bigger mediocre company.<br />Great companies don't spend time motivating people; they hire motivated people and create the conditions that secure commitment and continued motivation. Some companies kill it.<br />There were no big events that signaled transformation.<br />Some great companies are in terrible and unexciting industries, e.g. Philip Morris, yet perform remarkably well.<br /><br />The key characteristics of companies that moved from good to great:<br /><br />The leadership style requires personal humility and dogged professional will--\\"more like Lincoln and Socrates than Patton or Caesar.\\" Collins calls this \\"Level 5 Leadership.\\"<br />\\"First Who...Then What.\\" First get the right people on the bus in the right seats, and then figure out your vision and strategy of where you're going.<br />\\"Confront the Brutal Facts (Yet Never Lose Faith).\\"<br />\\"The Hedgehog Concept\\"--a crystal clear but simple blending of understanding \\"what you can be the best in the world at,\\" and \\"what you are deeply passionate about\\" plus what drives your economic engine? (This refers to the fable of the hedgehog and the fox. The fox knows many little things but the hedgehog know one big thing.)<br />\\"A Culture of Discipline.\\" You don't need hierarchy, bureaucracy and excessive controls if discipline is ingrained in the culture. This involves commitment to objectives but freedom to operate.<br />\\"Technology Accelerators\\"--Good-to-great companies are pioneers in the application of carefully selected technologies.<br />\\"The Flywheel\\"--The process resembles relentlessly pushing a giant heavy flywheel in one direction, turn upon turn, building momentum until a point of breakthrough, and beyond. There is no grand moment but success breeds success.<br /><br />With that as background, the monograph accompanying the book looked at the social sectors and \\"why business thinking is not the answer.\\" Collins sees parallels but notes some differences also, offering some excellent examples, which he discusses:<br /><br />Defining \\"Great\\"--calibrating success without business metrics, i.e. what is success? It can involve qualitative measurements, e.g. the Cleveland Orchestra.<br />\\"Leadership--getting things accomplished within a diffuse power structure, e.g. the Girl Scouts.<br />Getting the right people on the bus within social sector constraints, e.g. dealing with the teacher's union if you're trying to build a great school or department. Anecdotal and specific stories such as the Roger Briggs Story and the Wendy Kopp Story were offered.<br />Converting the economic engine (profit motive) to a resource engine (to fund the programs) in the Hedgehog Concept. \\"The foundation of doing good is doing well.\\"--Business guru Peter Drucker. An interesting example here: The Center for the Homeless in South Bend, Indiana where they rejected certain sources of funding that would have driven them away from their concept and diluted their service to the world.<br />Building momentum by building the brand--turning the flywheel. Collins offers several good examples. Collins finishes with a discussion of \\"systemic problems\\" as people see them. He doesn't discount them as meaningful but says \\"we can find pockets of greatness in nearly every difficult environment...Every institution has a unique set of irrational and difficult constraints, yet some make a leap while others facing the same environmental challenges do not.\\"<br /><br />His key point:<br /><br />\\"Greatness is not a function of circumstance. Greatness, it turns out, is largely a matter of conscious choice, and discipline.\\"<br /><br />This is a very forceful statement! Collins also says that great business organizations have more in common with great social sector organizations than they do with mediocre businesses. He says, \\"The key question is not business vs. social sector, but great vs. good.<br /><br />An excellent thought provoking and optimistic book.assionate about\\" plus what drives your economic engine? B004EPYZXK|4.0|When I saw this on opening day in a theater with about a dozen other people present, I found it to be both not as bad as I had feared, and not as good as I had hoped.  Credit must be given for the cleverness of combining the seemingly exhausted found-footage horror genre, with the realistic historic space exploration genre, while using a significant amount of real archival footage and using mostly period-accurate equipment to capture the new footage.  The result is something that costs very little to make, yet is fairly convincingly real more often than expected.  This is also clever in that it appeals in different ways to the established audiences for found-footage horror, science fiction, docudrama, and conspiracy theory drama audiences.  With just a modest initial weekend draw on each of those special-interest audiences, the film easily made back it's estimated 5 million dollar budget. Two big caveats which may be very specific to a very small population other than myself: I am a sucker for anything Apollo-related, and I don't care that the film was never for even a moment even slightly scary.  I don't go to movies to squeal like a schoolgirl at cheap scare tactics, so I was not disappointed when my pulse remained steady. And after all of that rather jaded criticism, I must say it really was an honest and mostly successful attempt to make an interesting and involving film.  No offense to the director, but this is very much a film created through a combination of many hours of largely improvised performances from good, understated actors, and a very fast-working and creative editing and audio post production team.  The only significant failure of this film was in not conveying any real sense of just how terrifying it must have been for the real Apollo astronauts, even on missions that went mostly by the book.  It is hard to imagine just how absolutely hostile to human life the environment outside of those tiny spacecraft and EVA suits really is.  The unusually lengthy selection of cut, extended, and alternate scenes on this DVD/Blu-Ray release are an enjoyable diversion, giving much insight into the process of making the film.gthy selection of cut, extended, and alternate scenes on this DVD/Blu-Ray release are an enjoyable diversion, giving much insight into the process of making the film.

We learn to over eat when stressed and anyone who has had a nicotine or gambling habit has learned to use these habits as crutches or amusements. So knowing that; What can we do about them?<br /><br />The author sheds some light on what will work and what won't.  First the habit is a series of Events: Routine, Reward, Cue.  Once we understand the habit we can adjust it.<br /><br />While this book is not so much a self-help on dealing with poor habits (but it helps), it is a primer for understanding what habits are. Understanding this we can learn to manage them and make them into better habits. If you think you know habits, think again, some of the information was very new to me.<br /><br />How companies respond to various events can make or break them. Much of this response is due to internal habits and an informal structure, you won't find in any new hire manual.  How do you get in there and change things?<br /><br />Similarly a society has all sorts of rules for its behavior.  Peer pressure is a form of a society's habitual behavior and this can change that same society in the process.<br /><br />I found this book hard to put down. The reason, it seems, that so many people are complaining about the lack of a widget is that on many devices there are installed in a location where they are not recognized.  I had to go to Manage Applications in the settings area.  Once there, I selected Weather+.  I noticed an option to move the app to the phone.  Once I did that, three Weather+ widgets appeared at the top of my widgets list.  But considering they are the same blocks of information you see in the pictures I was actually disappointed.  I would have preferred a small, simple one that provides the current conditions.  If I want current conditions by Weather+ on my homescreen I get a giant widget that takes up half the screen.  I use an alternate weather widget for current conditions and then the five day forecast by Weather+.  By the way, I primarily use this on a Droid X2.  On a tablet it is much nicer as all the available widgets can just reside in one corner of the screen (or however you prefer, of course).<br /><br />Second, with regard to the lack of updating, the visible differences in indicated weather from this to other apps, and the source of the weather.  I noticed that the widget on my home screen had shown the temperature had dropped 9 degrees while Weather+ had not yet reflected this change.  Browsing the settings I noticed that the default setting was to update every three hours.  The shortest duration available is currently one hour.  Upon changing to thatit reported the same temperature as the other app.  Likely they get their data from the same or similar sources.  I believe the app should update the weather whenever it is opened.  Seems like common sense especially when you consider that this app doesn't have a home screen widget.  You only see the weather it reports when you open it.  No point to open it more often than once an hour, at this point, if you are looking for changes.<br /><br />Third, there is a criticism of having to manually enter each city. It is also very beautiful on a tablet.  Criticizing the size of the app seems silly in this day and age.<br /><br />In conclusion, if the hourly update is a deal breaker for you then fine.
